B007N5YJWK|5.0|One reviewer of the Criterion edition accurately distinguishes between two kinds of audio commentary on film, one focused on the production of the film (anecdotes, gossip) that does not discus the film itself and another that does the reverse.  I happen to dislike the former and like the latter.  Rudy Belmer's audiocommentaries will tell you nothing about Curtiz's Adventures of Robin Hood or, in the Criterion edition, about Olivier's Richard III as a film.  For me, they are a complex waste of time.  I admire Criterion for having scholars who actually know how to pay attention to the film they are discussing how to discuss its form, as is the case with The 39 Steps.  If you don't pay attention to the canted angle of the first shot in which Donat appears, buying a ticket, you are missing something (something  Hitchcock learned when he went to UFA studios outside Berlin and watched German Expressionist films).  If you don't appreciate the fact that Tippi Hedren's face does not appear in Marnie until five minutes into the film (when we have seen her packing up, leaving her hotel room, and then heading to the train station, you are missing something.  No filmmaker deserves and rewards close formal attention more than Hitchcock.  Having a bright, critically informed film scholar direct your attention to what you are actually watching is a real treat.  I can read about a film's production history or about a film star or even about the director on wikipedia if I want.  Hearing someone talk about what is right in front of you as you watch it is invaluable and irreplaceable.

B006PTL1QM|5.0|Historic dramas have become quite popular in recent years, but I would have never imagined someone taking on Freud and Jung, particularly in this way.  It is a story mostly about Carl Jung's tumultuous relationship with Sabina Spielrein, with Freud as a secondary, albeit important figure in the film.  Vincent Cassel almost steals the show as the randy Otto Gross, who essentially pushes Jung into a relationship with Spielrein by encouraging him to follow his emotions.  This is more a dramatic vehicle than it is a study of psychoanalysis, probably more influenced by the method acting of Stanislavsky than it was the so-called \\"dangerous method\\" of Freud. As such, the acting is impeccable.  Viggo Mortensen gives a wonderfully deft touch to Freud, Michael Fassbender captures the repressed emotional state of Jung perfectly, and Keira Knightley gives a stand-out performance as Sabina Spielrein.  David Cronenberg uses letters to give the story an air of verisimilitude, but the story shouldn't be taken as fact.  It is more based on the [[ASIN:0571214851 stage production]] by Christopher Hampton, than it is the [[ASIN:0307950271 book]] by John Kerr.  As the film winds all too quickly to an end on the eve of World War I, it leaves you begging for more.<br /><br />What I liked most is that Cronenberg avoided reducing his historic figures to caricatures.  He reveals them to be the complicated individuals that they were, and that we can't hope to understand them.  What we can do is enjoy this little slice of their lives, as they intersected in early 20th century Europe.  Yet, Cronenberg touches on so many aspects on Freud's and Jung's psychoanalytical views, including dreams, as they sail to America together.  He provides a fascinating portrait of Sabina Spielrein, who was brought to Jung on the verge of complete emotional collapse. Through his calm counseling and eventually through their love affair would become a leading psychoanalyst herself, with the further tutelage of Freud. This was a tumultuous time with the advent not only of modern thinking that would  reshape science and the arts, but also of modern war, which would turn Europe on its end in World War I, alluded to by Jung's dream at the end of the film.  Like any great film, it leaves you wanting to know more about the historic figures involved.. This was a tumultuous time with the advent not only of modern thinking that would  reshape science and the arts, but also of modern war, which would turn Europe on its end in World War I, alluded to by Jung's dream at the end of the film. B006W0QOF2|4.0|Wow. Reading the reviews on Drive is like listening to Republicans and Democrats debate health care - Lots of noise and everybody seems to miss the point.<br /><br />Here's the deal.<br /><br />Drive is an unapologetic riff of pretty much every loner crime revenge story ever made and total line descendant of Michael Mann's Thief from 1980, . So much so, that thanks to the bubbly soundtrack, it seems they might as well have filmed it in 1980 too. Drive is a small-time crime story told with great restraint on characterizations and great explosiveness when it comes to violence. As far as modern influences go, Drive is a sort of crazy hybrid of Paul Verhoeven's Spetters and Paul Thomas Anderson's Hard Eight (Sydney) throw in the last few Cronenberg movies while you're at it.<br /><br />While I don't quite understand the negativity so many haters seem to have for Drive, I understand why some people could be so frustrated by it. Drive is told with Nicolas Winding Refn's European eye for detail and tone. Drive is the definition of character study with a heavy emphasis on STUDY. There is very little quick cutting between camera setups and absolutely zero sitcom-esque axial cut dialog scenes. It is the antithesis of the way most American movies are made. We have been conditioned as a society to watch boxes of talking heads broken up with pointless jabber and largely bloodless violence.  On the other hand, I find it hard to call Drive a masterpiece simply because the director knows how to hold a shot instead of a cut every 6 seconds.<br /><br />Now how you interpret Drive's way to tell a story is largely a question of style and sensibility. Drive is not for every taste and probably not something you'll be in the mood to watch 24/7, but it is very well done when you have the time to invest in it. While it breaks no new ground - and if you think it does, you must have been born yesterday - Drive is a really immersive odyssey of moments and music. It's essentially the cruise through LA crime movie Michael Mann tried to make with the Hit-Man centered Collateral.<br /><br />Drive is better.

The prose is overwrought and often bombastic, the overuse of descriptives is tedious, and much of the reconstructed dialogue is awkward and sounds unnatural.  In this respect, Luttrell was not well-served by his co-author/ghostwriter, Patrick Robinson.  LONE SURVIVOR also starts slow with a lot of fluff and a seemingly interminable C-130 flight from Bahrain to Bagram that spans three chapters. It might not be the most elegant read, but it is most definitely a worthwhile and satisfying one.

(Unlocked, the watch time seems to drift, gaining as much as +15 seconds a day the first three days I had it.  I was concerned that I'd have to send it back.)<br /><br />Once you have the watch locked down (typically, I wear the watch with pushers and crown locked), the alarm will be so soft as to be useless (and having a 12-hour alarm rather than a 24-hour one is really annoying). 0307719219|5.0|History is not destiny.<br /><br />So say Why Nations Fail authors Daron Acemoglu (Economics Professor at MIT and 2005 John Bates Clark Medal recipient) and James Robinson (Harvard political science and economics professor).<br /><br />According to Acemoglu and Robinson what answers the question of why nations fail is one of political policy, to wit: are your nation's political economic institutions extractive or inclusive? By extractive the authors point out countries like North Korea or many of the former British colonies (both before and after independence). By inclusive the authors point out countries like North Korea and other nations created inclusive economic institutions that enforced property rights, created a level playing field and encouraged investments in new technologies and skills.<br /><br />As Acemoglu and Robinson point out understanding why nations fail often requires a review of recent and not so recent history that is nation specific and focused on whether its particular history supported inclusiveness or suffered exclusiveness.<br /><br />As part of advancing their thesis, the authors specifically refute other theses. One of these refuted theses is that geography is destiny. Readers familiar with Jared Diamond's books Guns, Germs and Steel and Collapse may recognize the argument that countries from certain climates were destined for predominance over their less ideally located peers (a sort of economic \\"location, location, location\\" theory). Acemoglu and Robinson pour water on this theory by examing the case history of Korea which was united until just after World War II. Despite a common shared geographic, linguistic and cultural history up to that point, \\"somehow\\" South Koreans now outearn their northern neighbors at a rate of 400 to 1.<br /><br />Another these the authors dispute is culture which is also addressed by the Korean example and less significantly perhaps by the case history of Nogales Arizona/Nogales Sonora (the city is bisected by the Mexican US border). As with Korea Nogales had a common history up until it was taken over during the Gadsen purchase. As with Korea standard of living is radically different based not on culture but rather arbitrary border placement.<br /><br />Finally the authors dispute the ignorance hypothesis. This one says that some leaders simply don't know how to make their countries wealthy. Here again, an examination of the personal wealth and living standards of the Communist Party elite in North Korea fail to show any unfamiliarity with how to acquire money. Rather, the oppressive conditions in that country testify to the intensity of the desire on the part of Korea's rulers to maintain that standard of living exclusively for themselves even at the cost of the extreme suffering of their people.<br /><br />More than refuting other theories, however, the authors take readers on a world wind tour of global history from the perspective of economic inclusive. From my perspective whether you ultimately agree with the rest of what they say, this tour itself is alone worth the price of this book.<br /><br />But in this case, it's likely the authors are actually making a compelling case because it also happens to be the right one. From the axial age forward, every step towards a greater standard of living has always seemed to follow in the wake of a greater respect for the rights of the many...not total inclusiveness perhaps but greater inclusiveness to be sure. As Acemoglu and Robinson alluded there are indeed many details to be filled in but as a start I think this book makes for both provocative reading and cutting edge economics and political science.by the Mexican US border). As with Korea Nogales had a common history up until it was taken over during the Gadsen purchase. As with Korea standard of living is radically different based not on culture but rather arbitrary border placement.<br /><br />Finally the authors dispute the ignorance hypothesis. This one says that some leaders simply don't know how to make their countries wealthy. Here again, an examination of the personal wealth and living standards of the Communist Party elite in North Korea fail to show any unfamiliarity with how to acquire money. Rather, the oppressive conditions in that country testify to the intensity of the desire on the part of Korea's rulers to maintain that standard of living exclusively for themselves even at the cost of the extreme suffering of their people.<br /><br />More than refuting other theories, however, the authors take readers on a world wind tour of global history from the perspective of economic inclusive. From my perspective whether you ultimately agree with the rest of what they say, this tour itself is alone worth the price of this book.<br /><br />But in this case, it's likely the authors are actually making a compelling case because it also happens to be the right one. From the axial age forward, every step towards a greater standard of living has always seemed to follow in the wake of a greater respect for the rights of the many...not total inclusiveness perhaps but greater inclusiveness to be sure. As Acemoglu and Robinson alluded there are indeed many details to be filled in but as a start I think this book makes for both provocative reading and cutting edge economics and political science.

0515142247|4.0|Lee Child spins a terrific yarn that is entertaining, fast-paced and spiced with enough character delineation to keep modern readers interested. Die Trying is a rollercoaster of a suspense novel and if the reader has to work a little harder in spots to buy the action, the pace will sustain.<br /><br />Jack Reacher is ex-army, ex-military police. He was a decorated, upstanding officer who slipped through the downsizing cracks of the peace-time military establishment. Reacher is going no place fast now he's in civvies, but that isn't to imply he's become a bum, far from it. Because of his long service career and extensive overseas assignments, Reacher is taking his time and exploring civilian options. He's becoming acquainted with his country. That puts him in sudden jeopardy.<br /><br />In the wrong place at the wrong time, he's kidnapped with an FBI agent named Holly Johnson. In a fine twist, Reacher and the reader begin to suspect some unseen dimensions to this woman. That thread carries through for a good part of the novel and adds interesting dimensions to the main plot.<br /><br />In some fascinating ways, Jack Reacher is a throwback to the hard-bitten, stalwart, stand-up heroes of an earlier time. If he can maintain the pace and the level of good quality writing readers will find here, his scarred hero will live a long and fruitful life.

It discusses China, India, Brazil, Mexico, Russia, Poland, Czech Republic, Hungary, Bulgaria, Turkey, Sri Lanka, Thailand, Malaysia, Taiwan, Korea, Vietna, South Africa and includes a discussion of Africa as well.  Even more countries are covered but the above slightly more comprehensively with special chaperts dedicated to China, India, Brazil, Mexico, Russia and Turkey.  The author is responsible for investing in emerging markets for Morgan Stanley and is well positioned to give a narrative on most of EM.  The commentary is well thought out and the author does well to poke holes in some common misconceptions.  It is important to remember though that the author's perspectives are just his own beliefs about the future, most of what he writes can be debated vigorously.  I enjoyed reading the overviews as the author is concise and articulate, but this is but one of many perspectives and the author does not go into details to defend his views.<br /><br />Being responsible for important asset allocation at a major wealth management firm naturally means the author will have to take views and make decisions based on their beliefs and given their knowledge.  The author believes that much is at risk in Brazil, India, China, Mexico and Russia.  For him, he favours Korea which has managed to move very much into the category of innovative countries focusing on consumer demand, which the author makes the point of showing that consumer goods is where the long term growth is.  He focuses on distribution of wealth, where retained profits are being invested, what the governments are doing and the nature of their trade and reform agendas.  These commentaries are very straightforward to read and much information is included in a concise fashion.  The author discusses what has been working for economies and spends time considering what might go wrong for them in the future.<br /><br />Despite enjoying much of what was written, there is a fair amount of opinion that is marketed as fact and there are many points he makes that the author's own commentary discredit.  In particular the author has been making the argument that easy monerary policy is just fuelling speculative commodity demand and has been very detrimental in the recovery.  That is speculation, not fact and there is no counterfactual we can point to.  Commodity speculation might have been increasing but there has been fundamental demand growth in commodities due to Chinas focus on FAI growth.  Monetary policy no doubt can impact the price but the degree is not in the least bit obvious.  Another example of reasoning the can be discredited is the observation that growth in post war periods is higher than average (this is obvious isnt it?) and then makes the comment that markets dont price that growth aspect properly, several pages later there is another statement concerning the probability a nation goes back to war having recently emerged as being 40%.  That would explain why people find if hard to buy equity in countries recently emerging from war wouldnt it?  The author's narratives are fun to read and he debunks much that people talk about when it comes to emerging markets.  The economic commentary is suspect and much of what the author states with confidence is just his opinion for which many with much evidence strongly disagree.  Consider the example of Turkey, a country the author very much is a fan of which has recently been the target of the economist (after this publication) as well as the subject of a comprehensive GS piece in which on robust statistical measures it has significant risks or consider Korea which has large demographic issues which it faces and high personal debt levels which the author casually mentions.  Which is right, who knows, but the authors views are definitely not unbiased and one should not invest in his favorites without doing more research.

0345803485|4.0|After hearing so much about this book, I finally looked it up on Amazon. The preview of the first 8 pages were discouraging, as well as learning that the main characters were modeled after Bella and Edward of Twilight. However, I decided to reserve judgement until I'd read the whole novel. I found that I was pleasantly surprised, despite the numerous problems throughout the story.<br /><br />First, the bad:<br /><br />1. The novel is written in what I suppose is \\"stream-of-consciousness\\", however, I doubt that anyone begins every thought with \\"Holy (fill-in-the-blank)\\" and even if they did, it doesn't quite work in written form. It's repetitive and lessens the intensity of the story. Also, there's far too much eye-rolling considering the characters are all adults.<br /><br />2. There's a LOT that's ripped off from Twilight---Anastasia's blushing, lip-biting and self-esteem issues; her \\"harebrained\\" mother; Christian being extremely attractive (with untidy hair!) and limitless riches; him telling Anastasia that she should stay away (yet he keeps talking to her)---the list goes on.<br /><br />3. I get that Christian Grey is hot and very sexy but even so, Anastasia's \\"earth-shattering\\" orgasms happen far too easily and often in the same manner. Also, considering the subject matter and the number of f-bombs dropped in the novel, the sex scenes are generalized and contain silly lines like \\"he touched me...there.\\" Come on!<br /><br />4. There really isn't much of a plot--the major conflict of the story is whether or not Anastasia will sign the \\"contract\\" Christian writes out detailing the terms of their BDSM relationship. There's not a lot of character development either---with the exception of Christian Grey, who clearly struggles between what he's always known and the undeniable feelings he's developing for Anastasia.<br /><br />Now, the good:<br /><br />1. Despite the Twilight rip-offs, everything I hated about that book are absent here. For starters, Anastasia is a likeable character. Christian's controlling tendencies are not painted as romantic, in fact, he's quite upfront about this aspect of his personality and Ana is quick to call him out on it (even if it's in a tone of \\"You big meanie-head!\\"). Though it's her first relationship, she's aware enough to notice that it isn't quite how she pictured it would or should be. Anastasia becomes even more despondent as she watches her best friend become happy and carefree with Christian's brother---the exact opposite of herself. Unlike Bella, Anastasia is independant and wants a life outside of Christian.<br /><br />2. The emails between Christian and Anastasia are snarky and quite entertaining--well, for me anyway.<br /><br />3. Anastasia remarks often on Christian's hotness and Christian wants to bone Anastasia pretty much every second but you get the sense that there's genuine love blossoming between them. You find yourself rooting for the characters by the end.<br /><br />Fifty Shades of Grey has its flaws and it's not a work of literary genius. However, there's a certain pull to it and makes for a great rainy-day read. I would definitely recommend this book.

B0079J26TI|5.0|With the exception of the Schumann Symphony #4, the early Romantics were not often programmed by Klemperer in his concerts.  The performance of the Schumann Symphony #4 on this recording is nothing short of magnificent, one of the greatest recordings ever made of this work.<br /><br />The Mendelssohn performances are more controversal. The tempos for all the movements of the Mendelssohn Symphony #3 \\"Scottish\\" and the Symphony #4 \\"Italian\\" are slower than usual, as was typical of Klemperer in the early 1960's. The orchestral balance, phrasing, and mastery of structure, however, lend a satifying inevitability to each of these works. The \\"Scottish\\" in particular, which always struck me as being a bit episodic, has great cohesiveness in Klemperer's hands.  The same can be said for the companion Hebrides Overture. Klemperer, at least temporarily, convinces you that everybody else plays these works too fast.<br /><br />From a technical standpoint, the SACD remasterings give a sharper focus, resulting in lighter weight, and greater orchestral transparency than their redbook counterparts. Despite their age (1960), no apologies have to be made for the sound on these recordings.<br /><br />These performances may not be to everybody's tastes, but they are certainly worth listening to. After a few listenings, you too may fall under Klemperer's spell.

0307951529|5.0|This is, in short, a fantastic book. Chris Guillebeau--a self-taught entrepreneur who walks the talk in terms of having created from scratch a sustainable lifestyle business for himself--has collected dozens of case studies from other people who have done the same.<br /><br />What makes the book really compelling is that their stories are deftly woven into a step-by-step blueprint of how you can do the same; it's not simply a collection of anecdotes. The book covers idea generation, marketing, product launches, scaling your business, and more.<br /><br />Additionally, the case studies cover both a wide range of fields (technology, the arts, travel, food, and more) and a wide range of business types (brick-and-mortar to 100% online and everything in between). You will most likely find something in here that's highly relatable to your own fledgling idea, whatever it may be.<br /><br />Chris has also nailed down and shared income specifics for all of the profiled companies, which makes their stories much more interesting and instructive than simply telling us someone has started, say, \\"a successful travel-booking business.\\"<br /><br />Finally, while I have read and enjoyed books like The 4-Hour Workweek, I found this one much more directly applicable to my own life. I do not consider myself a born entrepreneur - many of the successful entrepreneurs profiled here stumbled into their work almost by accident (via unexpected layoff or hobby that grew into something more). You don't need to quit your day job and risk everything to make this work.<br /><br />Also, the book provides strong support for the idea that you really can start something lucrative without a huge initial investment - self-employment is not just for the trust-funded or those who are looking to raise lots of venture capital. Actual start-up costs are provided; the majority of those profiled, throwing out the outliers, spent under $500 getting everything up and running.<br /><br />Chris is a natural writer, and the book is a very interesting, compelling read - it's packed full of useful info and just a lot of fun besides. Highly recommended.

B0079R8IYW|2.0|The Marware Exchange Case Cover with Stand for Kindle Fire<br /><br />Let's get to the point: I hate this thing.<br /><br />Here's why:<br /><br />1. It looks cheap.<br /><br />The case is made of a thin plastic sheeting, grained to look like leather. It doesn't look like leather. It looks like what it is: ugly stitched plastic. To use a phrase that is au courant: \\"this thing is ghetto\\"; as in it looks like something you got from a street-vendor, after you haggled for a while.<br /><br />Holding it reminds you of that old commercial with the catchy fake rock tune: \\"The back of my legs, stickin' to the pleatheeeeer...\\"<br /><br />2. Its design is a crowded gathering of bad ideas.<br /><br />The Marware Exchange Case Cover with Stand for Kindle Fire<br /> is a case with an integrated stand only here, it just isn't done well.<br /><br />Its stand  works by folding the cover backwards over a couple of hinged folds and sticking the end into a tab sewn to the back of the cover. You have to pry up the notch to get the end of the cover under it (with possible long-term wear issues that will make it look worse faster), but there's another blunderiffic factor in play: the Marware Exchange Case Cover has pockets and slots designed for it to be used as a card-holder and wallet.<br /><br />And it is at this point that you wonder if its design is actually not a mistake but an accidental import intended for Krypton, Superman's home planet, where there was no poverty and no crime...<br /><br />Here on earth, the case-as-wallet idea is bad on a lot of levels, the least frightening of which is putting all your eggs in one basket and then showing everyone how valuable they are. It means that you are a one-stop shopping center for thieves who specialize in stealing from people in cafes; it means, if you have a bad day, and your case vanishes, the creep who took it gets it all--a nice, sellable Amazon Kindle, your credit cards, your cash, your business cards with your name and address on them...<br /><br />But wait, there's more!<br /><br />The cash pocket is shorter than a dollar bill is tall showing the top edges of the bills when the case is open and putting your credit/business cards in plain sight. Better still, when you use the stand, not only are all your valuables visible, but the bill-pocket is upside-down.<br /><br />Maybe this thing wasn't designed for Krypton: maybe it was cleverly designed for police sting operations to entrap thieves using an irresistable target stuffed with cash and credit cards.<br /><br />All told, this is weak product design at its worst--a collection of bad ideas that trip over one another to see which one can bite you first.<br /><br />3. Its soft magnet closure eliminates the need for an elastic strap that you can put over the back of your hand to help hold it.<br /><br />Now it's true; reading on an Amazon Kindle isn't so hard they made it part of Navy SEAL training, but like all tablets and readers, how you hold it is important because it can make  the reading experience better, and putting your hand under a strap makes things easier. The Marware Exchange Case Cover's approach removes a sound and useful feature.<br /><br />4. The bottom line: this thing is just plain bad.<br /><br />You can do better for your money. Marware makes other, more ambitious designs that look better, have better stands, provide better ergonomics and that close with an elastic band that doubles as a hand-strap.<br /><br />Buy one of them. They will make you happier.

0525478817|4.0|Before I get into the meat of my criticisms, let me start by saying that The Fault in Our Stars by John Green is a beautiful, emotionally touching book.  It's identified as a young adult novel, but I wouldn't hesitate to recommend it to just about anyone.  It's a worthwhile read.<br /><br />But as good as the book can be, there are a couple of faults worth noting.  The first of which is John Green's voice for lead character Hazel Grace.  She's written as a sixteen year old girl, yet her vocabulary far exceeds that of a moderately intelligent adult.  Reading some of the dialog between her and her boyfriend Augustus felt like watching an episode of Gilmore Girls.  That's not a good thing.<br /><br />The relationship between Hazel and Augustus is a believable one.  They rely on each other for strength as they both cope with having cancer.  It's refreshing that both characters recognize the hopelessness of their situations, and reflect on life rather than blame the world.<br /><br />But towards the end of the book, a series of completely unbelievable events occur.  From the use of the Make A Wish Foundation, to the wacky actions of an alcoholic author, things just aren't believable.  These events take the reader out of the story, and moments which should tug on your heartstrings do less so because of them.  It's a shame, because without these moments, The Fault in Our Stars is near perfect in creating a bond between the reader and the characters.

1469984202|5.0|I have been reading science fiction since 1966 and if I were to rank the 1000+ books and stories I have read in the genre, I would have to place Wool in my top five.<br /><br />I will begin with the obligatory (or, at least, courteous) SPOILER ALERT, on the off chance that a potential purchaser who reads five star reviews (I don't) will choose to read mine over the other 600+ five star ones that have been written to date. Personally, I ignore five star reviews when shopping for books since I assume they were written by the author's friends (although, in this case, OK, I get that nobody has 600 friends, at least not 600 friends willing to write a review.) Instead I read the one and two star reviews to see what it was that made the reader dislike the story to decide if that issue would bother me too. More on that later.<br /><br />Anyway, having protected the potential buyer from the trauma of spoilers, I will address the rest of this review to the author.<br /><br />Dear Hugh,<br />I am very glad you sold this as a collection. I needed the hope that with 93% of the collection to go, you would make it up to me for making me care so deeply about Holston that I mourned for him when he died. (Yes, potential reader who has chosen to ignore the spoiler alert, this author makes you care deeply about several characters and then kills them off!) I also loved and then mourned Mayor Jahns in Wool 2. Ditto for Deputy Marnes in Wool 3. And you horrified me by starting Wool 3 with Juliette in the airlock! But at that point there was 79% of the work to go so I was hopeful...and completely hooked.<br /><br />Hugh, you are a master at making people care about your characters to the point that the readers feel as if they have actually lost a friend when the character dies.<br /><br />I don't recall what made me add this to my Kindle collection, let alone what made me purchase it. All but 28 of the 330 books I have on my Kindle were free; and of the 28 I paid for, 27 were by authors whose books I had previously bought in hardcover or paperback.  It may have been that WOOL showed up repeatedly in a blog I follow and while I was reading the 1 & 2 star reviews I could not help but notice the sheer quantity of positive reviews. So between the intriguing description and the plethora of stars, I took a chance...... and struck gold!<br /><br />Now about those 1 & 2 star complaints: yes, I skimmed parts I knew would keep me from sleeping (I have horror of drowning) and I skimmed battles knowing that characters I had come to care about would die because that's what happens in a rebellion, but that was me protecting myself from emotional overload. So, should you shorten those parts? No. The story does not need to be \\"moved along.\\" It's not a half hour TV sitcom. When events play out almost in real time, it adds to the desperation of the situation.<br /><br />Since there were so few ones and twos, I read some threes and I disagree with some criticisms there. One reader insists that there would have been elevators, but most people in the silo, having been told all of their lives by the priests that God had created the silo, would never even think of challenging that; and if someone with mechanical aptitude did think of it and pushed to be allowed to build one, they would be killed- as George was when he pushed for using the diggers.<br /><br />Another criticized the lack of a murder investigation. That was covered. It would have been pointless- Marnes realized who held the power. He would not have been able to bring the killer to justice.<br /><br />I have never written a review so I imagine I am way over the word limit; this may end up as a letter instead, but I wanted to add that Lukas is not good enough for Juliette. I admired how you put Lukas in a position where he is learning about how the silo societies came to be and how they are designed to function so that the reader can learn the back story too, but had you killed him off I would not have mourned.Juliette cares about him, though, and he can be useful to her in continuing to research the truth so she can share the truth with the inhabitants of the silo, so OK, he gets to live.....but, I repeat, Juliette deserves better. Later when you explore the other silos and a character emerges who is worthy of her, you may kill off Lukas.<br /><br />Looking forward to buying and reading more of your silo stories, so get busy.<br />Sincerely,<br />Connie

1416914285|4.0|***NOTE MY REVIEWS OFTEN CONTAIN SPOILERS***<br /><br />Clary Frays is just your average ordinary New York City teen, that is until the night she sees a boy murdered in a local night club.  She sees his body - blue black blood and then he disappears all together and she learns that she didn't witness the death of a boy, but the death of a Demon.  Jace Wayland is a Shadow Hunter, one of the nephillim and the idea of a mundane girl who can see through glamour fascinates him, but when he returns to investigate more, he learns Clary is not what she seems.  When he mother is kidnapped and she herself attacked by a demon he's the only one who can help her.  Clary is soon immersed in a world of Shadow Hunters, Downworlders and Demons in the search of a past she never knew she had.  But it's her mother's secrets and her mother's past that brought this danger to her life.  And it's those secrets that might not only kill her mother, but her too.  Can Clary find the answers she seeks and save her mother before it's too late for them all?<br /><br />This is my second time reading this novel.  I opened it again after I received an audio copy as a gift and figured listening to his might help me sleep.  It didn't help me sleep, only hooked me on the story all over again and left me too impatient to reach its end to finish listening to the story.  I've discovered I have no patience for audio books, they read entirely too slow for me.  Thankfully I had a hard cover copy already on my shelf.<br /><br />While the story did still hook me a second time, I also found I was able to distance myself and see flaws that I hadn't noticed before.  First the set up for this story bears a striking resemblance to Harry Potter.  She's a child whose been lied to about what she is, where she comes from and exactly what exists in the world around her.  Like Harry Potter, the creatures of the Mortal Instruments series exist among the world of humanity, but hidden from them through glamours.  While each creature has a different Mythology than Rowling's world, and this story itself is set in the US instead of the UK, the amount of similarities between the stories are astounding once you start noticing them. Are they carbon copies? No, not by any means, but they are similar enough in set up that it bears mentioning.  Clary is not the warrior rushing into danger prepared to take down any enemy as Harry Potter is.  And while Clary has abilities she never knew of she can by no means be described as the chosen one, nor does she stand apart as of yet from others of her kind due to uncommon abilities.  The story is also written to appeal to an older audience than Harry Potter because Clary is a teenage girl who has many of the interests, worries and attractions of your average teenage girl.<br /><br />The story also encompasses what I'd guess you'd call a love quintet that Clare throws a major sick twist into at the end of the novel.  And just for good measure there's a past love triangle thrown into the mix.  You have Clary's mother who was loved by two men - one good and one evil, though the darkness in either man was not evident when this occurred.  So she befriended the first and loved the second and in some ways that love triangle spiraled half the current events in the story.  Then our male lead Jace has at the very least two people attracted to if not in love with him.  Our female lead Clary has the same, one of which of course is the male lead.  And then we've got two additional characters thrown in attracted to each of the third wheels in both love triangles.  Love wise, this story is in a way, a confusing dramatic mess that speaks to the teenage girl in the heart of every woman.<br /><br />Between the fantasy, romantic and dramatic elements despite it similarities to other stories Clare has woven a story that's difficult to put down even on the second reading.  The story is fast paced with some sort of action packed dramatic moment on practically every page.  There's no denying the book is a well written page turner and when you think about it, it's not creating something extremely original plot that makes a good book, it's taking a plot and owning it.  What I mean by that is bringing your own voice and unforgettable characters to a plot and adding your own twists and turns to the journey.  It's taking a plot that's been seen and managing to make your own version memorable which is the mark of a good book and a talented writer.<br /><br />Though the characters have decent development, in this novel its makes clear that we've only seen the tip of the iceberg with who these characters really are in City of Bones.  Clare gives us enough to become attached to them, but in writing this kind of reminds us that you don't get to know anyone inside and out in a matter of days so she isn't going to allow you that luxury with her characters either.  None of the characters introduced in City of Bones are one dimensional and though I can't say I feel like I know everything about any of them they leap from the page with their different forms of inner turmoil and strife.  What she gives us of these characters leave a reader itching to know more and considering this is the first in a series of novels that's a good thing.<br /><br />Overall I'd definitely recommend this book to anyone who enjoys young adult romance or urban fantasy.  While I can't say it will appeal to all Harry Potter fans, I can say that many of the same fans will embrace Clary and Jace, aching for them in the same way they spend the last more than ten years aching and feeling for the boy who lived.ere's no denying the book is a well written page turner and when you think about it, it's not creating something extremely original plot that makes a good book, it's taking a plot and owning it. Her lack of footnotes is especially unsettling.  Footnotes, for those who seem to believe everything they read on the net, are crucial for separating fact from unsubstantiated rumor.  I have seen the sentence about \\"sons being forced to rape their mothers\\" in this book and the net, but not one footnote to substantiate it from fiction.  There are also a great many inconsistencies in this book.  For instance the author writes about how the women were killed after being raped, but later in the book muses about the many possible pregnancies of half Japanese children and suicides from these pregnancies.  She also states that no women was safe from rape regardless of age, but then later recounts how one girl escaped being raped by dressing as an old woman.  One of the few stories which she does seem to have documentation for she tells twice.  The authors' lack of chronological order for this book also makes it hard to read.  She does much better when dealing with authenticated third party information such as the written diary of Rabe.  I don't doubt that a great many of the things in this book are true, but to get a more objective account of what went on it might be better to read the diary of Rabe or some other scholarly piece of work on this subject.  I can not recommend this book as it falls into the category of sensational journalism (which by the way, was the authors' major - not history)

B002Z7FWBI|5.0|DIRTY HARRY has stood the test of time and remains a classic film based on the mores of a modern society tempered with logical judgment of how to dispense justice to those unlawful individuals in that society. Clint Eastwood as Dirty Harry is the personification of that tool of logical judgment much to the disdain of his superiors and to the legal system he works within. DIRTY HARRY and SUDDEN IMPACT are the two films that bring the motivations and morality of this character under close scrutiny as he comes full circle with a rather dichotomous approach to his job over the span of time of these two films. When I first saw SUDDEN IMPACT I thought Clint Eastwood had somehow sold out on the underlying essence of the character he had been portraying. He took the realistic base from the first three DIRTY HARRY films (DIRTY HARRY, MAGNUM FORCE and THE ENFORCER) and replaced it with some type of pseudo surrealistic parody that seemed so repulsive it bordered on bad taste to say the very least. However, after repeated viewing it started to grow on me and I actually wanted to watch it over and over. I couldn't get enough of it. It certainly was not the same Dirty Harry but it had a very blunt approach for dealing with crime and criminals. In the first three films the bureaucrats constantly accused Harry of crossing the line yet the audience knew Harry was right and the bureaucrats were just a bunch of crybaby liberals (at least that's what the directors wanted us to think). In SUDDEN IMPACT Clint Eastwood is at the helm. Eastwood proposes a question to the audience: You like crazy? Do you want to see crazy? I'm going to give you crazy. Film making is a business and like a good businessman Eastwood gives his audience what they want. Yet Eastwood is no fool. Without artistic merit this film would have been a failure. Eastwood is playing the auteur filmmaker here and he is very successful at it. This film is way ahead of its time. The fabric of a sane society is being pulled apart. Family values, if you like, are out the window. Eastwood collects the most vile, repugnant and repulsive villains for this film that literally defines the meaning of trailer trash. The Mafia hoodlums at the beginning of the film look like... choirboys compared to the bunch of wacko amusement park crazies he ends up exterminating by the film's end. If Eastwood had not directed this film with his offbeat humor and unconventional style, if he had played it as a straight drama, the critics would have screamed murder. SUDDEN IMPACT is a good film. It's a stand-alone modern day apocalyptic tale delivered for your approval by Dirty Harry. Looking at MAGNUM FORCE there is an attempt to justify the true motivations of the character but that film is not successful thematically and is rather heavy handed. THE ENFORCER's straight forward approach is more satisfying. THE DEAD POOL is a rather lethargic almost sentimental look back at Harry and perhaps he has been assimilated by the very system he operated in. That system no longer resembles that which it was. Yet Harry has some of that starch still left in him. I can still remember Reni Santoni asking in the original DIRTY HARRY, \\"Why do they call you Dirty Harry?\\" Well, seems like ages ago, but we know the answer.art. 0439023483|5.0|About the Book:<br />In a dystopian society, the Capital controls the districts around it by forcing strict rules upon them such as no hunting, no weapons, and restrictions on what sort of jobs they can have. However, the worst thing the districts are forced into is something called the Hunger Games. In these games, a boy and a girl from each district are chosen to participate and then thrown into an arena until only one is left alive. It is in these games that Katniss finds herself, and she is doing whatever it takes to survive.<br /><br />Review:<br />First off, I love the fact that Suzanne Collins does not shy away from heavy issues and violence even though this is a young adult series. I love the fact that she believes that even teenagers can handle such issues (at least in text). With that being said, time to turn my thoughts to other aspects of this book.<br /><br />I have to admit that this was a page turner, even though it was fairly obvious what was going to happen at the end of this book. The question was how. I thoroughly enjoyed getting to see how Katniss got out of each scenario. I have to say that some of my favourite scenes took place in the Capitol. Yes, the actual Hunger Games were quite entertaining with its weapons and survival, but the Capitol is just as harsh. Just in a different sense. In the Capitol, we get to see the tributes battle the press and learn how to be a good celebrity so that they can obtain sponsors, which can help them better survive in the arena.<br /><br />Besides the entertainment value, Collins does a fabulous job at poking fun at our current society (which I thought was a nice underlayer) such as all the fashion trends that can be so outrageous as to almost be grotesque. Then, of course, there are the Hunger Games that the Capitol people watch religiously like our own American Idol.<br /><br />Collins also manages to create fascinating characters. Honestly, I cannot think of a single character I dislike. I may dislike that character as a person or be aggravated by some of their traits though. For example, I could not see myself getting along with Katniss, but she made an excellent character. Then again, Katniss isn't supposed to be liked by everyone. Some love her and other times she simple rubs people the wrong way, and that is how I felt about her throughout.<br /><br />Not only does Collins create interesting characters but boy does she have a knack for getting images and emotions to stick in your head for days upon end. Don't worry. I don't plan on giving out spoilers, but I have to say one major part that is still with me is the climax of the actual Hunger Games with the mutts (genetically altered animals). There was definitely a creep factor when they came into play at the end.<br /><br />To be honest, the only things I really disliked  was the present tense writing and the love triangle. I'm guessing the former is more of a personal choice, but I am sure plenty can agree with me that the love triangle is getting old. However, it was definitely a subplot and not the main focus so I did not feel like I was drowning in the trope.<br /><br />So all together, I felt that this was an excellent read. It had an entertaining plot, well-rounded characters, and it did a fantastic job at integrating our current society in a heavy yet satirical way. Collins has a gift, and I would recommend this to many people. In fact, if you haven't read this, I suggest doing so (with the next two books on hand, because you will be wanting them).

The Unholy Trinity of 1986 is a trio of thrash metal albums that came out in 1986 that have been highly revered for their sheer brutality and/or breakneck-fast riffs.  The trinity consists of Slayer's \\"Reign in Blood,\\" Kreator's \\"Pleasure to Kill,\\" and Dark Angel's \\"Darkness Descends.\\"  While \\"Reign in Blood\\" is the best-known of the three, I personally think it's the weakest of the bunch, though I still like it a lot.<br /><br />What can be said about Slayer's \\"Reign in Blood\\" that hasn't been said already?  It's a thrash metal album that's considered by many metalheads to be among the best and most intense ever made.  For a little while, I felt the same way, thought those sentiments would soon be dissolved once I sunk my teeth into the prime works of Kreator, Sodom, Morbid Saint, Dark Angel, and Pestilence (though they're known for being death metal, Pest' started off as a thrash metal band, and a superb one at that), just to name a few.<br /><br />MUSICIANSHIP<br /><br />I'll be honest here and say that while Slayer displays a good deal of confidence in their songwriting and mastery of instruments on this album, I felt the band saw better days in these areas when they recorded albums like \\"Hell Awaits,\\" \\"South of Heaven,\\" and \\"Seasons in the Abyss.\\"<br /><br />With the guitars, it's business as usual for Kerry King and Jeff Hanneman.  This album is loaded with tight, fast thrash riffs and \\"bunny in a blender\\" guitar solos.  While this works for the most part, in songs like \\"Piece by Piece,\\" it feels rather uninspired, especially since a lot of the songs are pretty short.  However, it works really well with some songs like \\"Necrophobic.\\"  \\"Angel of Death,\\" the most popular song on the album, displays probably the best dynamics in the guitar department, though some welcome time changes do occur in songs like \\"Postmortem\\" and \\"Raining Blood.\\"<br /><br />Dave Lombardo is widely-acclaimed for his drumming skills, and while I don't think he's one of the absolute best drummers in the heavy metal genre, his contributions on this album are really tight and fit the type of thrash on display here.  I think some of his best-sounding work in on the song \\"Reborn.\\"<br /><br />Tom Araya's vocals fit this album like a glove.  His vocals have a gruff feeling to them, adding a harsher edge to the music.  He also can dish out some blood-chilling screams like in the opening to \\"Angel of Death,\\" the near end of \\"Necrophobic,\\" and the middle of \\"Postmortem.\\"  I wish Araya's basslines were more audible in this album, since in previous Slayer recordings, they had more presence.<br /><br />SONGS<br /><br />Let's start off with the opening track, \\"Angel of Death.\\"  For those not in the know, this song is about one of the most evil men that ever lived, Joseph Mengele, a Nazi physician in Auschwitz infamous for his extremely twisted medical experiments on the camp's prisoners.  The lyrics about Mengele are a perfect fit for this twisted, iconic thrash song.  The highlights of \\"Angel of Death\\" are the really twisted dual guitar solos between King and Hanneman.  While \\"Reign in Blood\\" is well-known for being a fast and intense thrash album, it can utilize some good time changes to make the songs more interesting and even more intimidating than if it were just all fast.  As stated before, the last two songs, \\"Postmortem\\" and \\"Raining Blood\\" are a welcome change in the album and a great way to cap it off.  With \\"Raining Blood,\\" the slow, quiet introduction is a great way to start it off as it rather quickly turns into a chilling, intense thrash song.  The \\"fast\\" songs like \\"Necrophobic\\" can be really creepy and intense, and especially such track, as that one still still creeps me out for its brutal riffage, chilling guitar solo, and nefarious lyrics meticulously detailing torture and killing all in 1 minute and 40 seconds.The only \\"bad\\" song on here is \\"Piece by Piece, as it feels so flat and uninspired.  I'd say songs like \\"Altar of Sacrifice\\" and \\"Jesus Saves\\" are good, though not masterful.<br /><br />There's two bonus tracks, which is a re-recording of \\"Aggressive Perfector\\" and a remix of \\"Criminally Insane.\\"  While they're not bad, I prefer the original \\"Aggressive Perfector's\\" NWOBHM aesthetics that would have made it a perfect addition to Slayer's debut \\"Show No Mercy.\\"  The remix of \\"Criminally Insane\\" isn't bad, though I think the original feels much better.<br /><br />PRODUCTION<br /><br />The production on this album isn't bad, since the guitars, drums, and vocals come in really clear.  There's also a \\"harsh\\" aesthetic to the guitar sound, which is a good thing, especially for a thrash album.  I wish the bass had a stronger presence in the album, since I feel a well-mixed bass guitar can add some great power to the guitars and drums.<br /><br />FINAL WORD<br /><br />If you're fairly new to thrash metal and are curious about the more \\"intense\\" albums out there (especially with the Unholy Trinity of 1986), I suggest you start off with \\"Reign in Blood\\" first.  After you digest that album, then you're ready to tackle RIB's more intense counterparts, \\"Pleasure to Kill\\" and \\"Darkness Descends.\\"<br /><br />If you want Slayer's best work, check out \\"Hell Awaits,\\" \\"South of Heaven,\\" and \\"Seasons of the Abyss.\\"<br /><br />Personally, after getting into thrash albums like this, it made me rapidly lose any love I had for Metallica's \\"Master of Puppets.\\"The only \\"bad\\" song on here is \\"Piece by Piece, as it feels so flat and uninspired.  I'd say songs like \\"Altar of Sacrifice\\" and \\"Jesus Saves\\" are good, though not masterful.<br /><br />There's two bonus tracks, which is a re-recording of \\"Aggressive Perfector\\" and a remix of \\"Criminally Insane.\\"  While they're not bad, I prefer the original \\"Aggressive Perfector's\\" NWOBHM aesthetics that would have made it a perfect addition to Slayer's debut \\"Show No Mercy.\\"  The remix of \\"Criminally Insane\\" isn't bad, though I think the original feels much better.<br /><br />PRODUCTION<br /><br />The production on this album isn't bad, since the guitars, drums, and vocals come in really clear.  There's also a \\"harsh\\" aesthetic to the guitar sound, which is a good thing, especially for a thrash album.  I wish the bass had a stronger presence in the album, since I feel a well-mixed bass guitar can add some great power to the guitars and drums.<br /><br />FINAL WORD<br /><br />If you're fairly new to thrash metal and are curious about the more \\"intense\\" albums out there (especially with the Unholy Trinity of 1986), I suggest you start off with \\"Reign in Blood\\" first. B007IE4DSG|5.0|These days, the market seems awash with reissues containing demos and out-takes that were never intended (and barely fit) for public consumption.  Most of them are good for curiosity value, but don't reward repeated listening.  I'm pleased to say that this is not one of those collections.<br /><br />In the liner notes of the 30th Anniversary edition of [[ASIN:B00005214X All Things Must Pass]], George wrote that he thought the production was embarrassingly overblown and was very tempted to go back and remix it all but in the end, he decided to release it in the form that people were familiar with.  What we have on Early Takes is what we might have heard had George decided to strip back the arrangements.<br /><br />With the possible exception of My Sweet Lord, there is very little here that sounds unfinished.  Instead, it's more like what the songs would sound like had Unplugged been around in the early 70s.  I'd Have You Any Time is definitely superior to the album version and Woman Don't You Cry for Me (one of the few selections not taken from the All Things Must Pass era) is an excellent solo bluegrass piece - a more timeless arrangement than the funk version that was eventually released on [[ASIN:B00014TJ6G Thirty Three & 1/3]].<br /><br />Early Takes is a short album, only ten tracks and 30 minutes long, and priced accordingly.  Executive producer Olivia Harrison and Compilation producer Giles Martin have avoided the temptation to fill a disc with everything they can possibly find in the vault.  As such, it's a fitting, respectful and truly intimate glimpse at the early directions of some of George's songs.  Far from being a fans-only curio, it's an excellent album in its own right.

During 2010 and 2011, Madonna went on a very long hiatus to focus on other ventures like directing the movie 'W.E' but she was constantly writing new songs and eventually announced she was longing to make new music and looking for the sickest producers to work with in late 2010. In July 2011, it was confirmed she started recording songs for a new album to be released in 2012. In November 2011, a demo of a new song called 'Give Me All Your Love' leaked on the Internet to a lot of controversy, with very diverse reviews with people loving and hating the songs and a man ending arrested for leaking the demo. B004UC4HCG|5.0|For those unaware of what \\"barebones\\" means, the only thing this system needs is a CPU and memory to get working.  A hard drive helps, but in theory you can run it off a flash disk.  This is a case, power supply and motherboard.  The only video connectors are DVI and HDMI, if your monitor isn't equipped for one of those you'll want a DVI to VGA adapter.<br /><br />For a compact system it's surprisingly feature rich with lots of internal SATA ports.  Layout is reasonable, a little cramped but it's a tiny computer.  The only minor negative is that the hard drive cage is not a tool-less removal (the case and fan are thumbscrews, the hard drive cage uses low-profile screws).  Certainly not annoying enough to ding a star since building the system is probably a once-and-done setup for me.<br /><br />I'm running the cheapest CPU I could find in it, upgrading from a laptop approaching a decade old it'll breeze through whatever I want to do with it.  After a minor SNAFU regarding the age of memory I scavenged from an old system, I've now got 8GB of RAM.  This is a HTPC with a wireless keyboard/trackpad and Mythbuntu Precise loaded up cleanly with no additional drivers needed.  The only thing that was broken was the audio out of the HDMI port, which is fixed easily with this command:<br /><br />echo \\" pcm.!default {<br />  type hw<br />  card 0<br />  device 3<br /> }\\" >> ~/.asoundr<br /><br />You may also need to enable it via alsa-mixer.<br /><br />The system is quiet and may even be running cooler than the ancient laptop it replaced.  Plus the native HDMI support mean I can actually watch stuff using my full screen at 1080p.

Darth Vader and son  was funnier and much more touching than I expected. I plan on buying several copies to give as gifts, especially for expectant fathers. It would also make a timely Father's Day present.<br /><br />Men in this age bracket who grew up in the Seventies and now have children of their own will appreciate the father-son dynamic here. Lord Vader's dark reputation is very much softened as he raises young Luke as a single parent, apparently, affixing band-aids and playing checkers (\\"You have learned much, young one.\\"). The positive vibe isn't an accident. Author-illustrator Brown admitted, \\"With my son being 4, I thought it would be really fun to do something along the lines of Luke being a 4-year-old and Darth Vader going through the same things I was going through as a parent.\\"<br /><br />That perspective results in some LOL moments, for example:<br /><br />**When I was your age, we didn't even have star destroyers.**<br /><br />**Luke, pick up your toys this instant. Luke, I am your father. Do you want a time-out?**<br /><br />**Luke complains, \\"I don't want a sister,\\" as Leia plays nearby (and puts Luke's action figures in dresses).**<br /><br />Quality construction on the hardcover itself. Chronicle continues to publish wonderfully offbeat titles. Not sure why Amazon currently shows availability as \\"Usually ships within 1 to 3 months.\\" FYI, it's available (albeit at full price) at Chronicle's own site.<br /><br />[The reviewer was provided with a complimentary copy of the book.]

1416589473|4.0|\\"This is your chance, Dougal,\\" said Riona. \\"We go our separate ways. You go back to Divinity's Reach or Lion's Arch or wherever. You survived Ascalon City - twice. You have gems and gold to prove it. And while the Charr legions tear each other apart, you can just wait for the dust to settle and for the humans to come back. All you have to do is walk away.\\"<br /><br />  \\"I'm afraid I can't do that,\\" said Dougal. \\"You know that.\\"<br /><br />  Guild Wars: Ghosts of Ascalon, is a fairly short, high fantasy novel set in the fictitious game world of Tyria. It is a story about a human soldier turned thief named Dougal whose claim to fame, as the excerpt above states, is surviving Ascalon City. This is a dead city, haunted by the ghosts of the humans who at one time defended it from charr invaders and lost. Ghosts of Ascalon is a typical high fantasy story about a group of adventurers with a mission to do something impossible and of course only Dougal, the main character, can get it done.  Dougal is contacted by a group called the Vigil and they ask him to go back to Ascalon City to find a fabled weapon that if returned to their rightful owners may very well stop a costly war. The world has bigger problems with the recent rise of the ancient dragons and the five races of Tyria need to come together to face this new threat. Dougal is forced to face his past, find a way to get a group that couldn't be more different to work together and trust one another, and eventually the betrayal of an old friend.<br /><br />  While this is the back drop to the book, the main purpose of the book is to help bridge the gap between the online game Guild Wars 1 and 2. As there is a 250 year gap between both games the developer felt that there needed to be some explanation as to what happened between the first and the second game. As a fantasy novel the book is good, but I wouldn't say great or epic. As a book of lore for a video game the book is excellent. So those who will get the most out ofthis book will be those looking to understand more of the Guild Wars 2 lore before going to play the game.  Lore in a game is more important to some than others. For me lore is important because I like to create and play characters that really fit the world and could conceivably be found there. Knowing what has happened in the world and what is happening helps me to do this.<br /><br />  The writing and the pacing of the book are excellent. You get to know the main characters in a fairly quick manner, but you do feel like you know them at least a little. As this book's purpose is to focus on the lore of the game world each of the five races of Tyria, the sylvari, charr, humans, norn, and asura are represented in the party that goes to assist Dougal in his task. Each of the races has their particular mannerisms and outlooks on life. One thing that the book did was help to give an understanding of how each of the races are different and why perhaps they have a hard time coming together. There are wounds from the past, particularly between the charr and humans, who have been in a war that has been at a stalemate for over a decade and there are the typical issues that one finds when differing cultures come together. It goes into a bit of the world history which players of the first game will recognize and it also talks about the current state of the world and why the mission these adventurers are embarking on is important to the game world.<br /><br />  As mentioned above, the book is short and as far as fantasy goes there is nothing new here. Swords, sorcery, fantastical races, dragons, fighting, adventure. The story itself is also not particularly new for this genre, but the characters made it enjoyable and as I read it for the lore more than anything I found that I was not disappointed and I refer back to this often when discussing lore topics with other players.  It did help me to immerse into the world more deeply and I know I'll bring an even better character tolife in the game for having read it.

Suicide, to be more specific, is what is first encountered as you enter Murakami's world of pain and sorrow, but also patience, love, and hope. The book tries to highlight the ability to reminisce over the good in, possibly, some of our most difficult moments in life. Sometimes through humor, sometimes through sexual encounters, and sometimes by escaping with a ludicrous friend who just wants to get ahead in life because he gets a thrill through the 'game.' I should emphasize, there is a ubiquitous nature of sexuality that seems to suggest is at the 'crux of all human engagement in life.<br /><br />When I first began reading this novel, I was blown away. I have not yet experienced Murakami's world of writing until now. He is absolute genius. He stands in a category all of his own. His writing is simple, yet so heart-felt, so honest, so true. It cuts through you right to the heart, right to your spirit, right to your soul. I felt sad. I felt happy. I laughed. I went through all kinds of colorful emotions while reading this novel. I felt the defeat, and I also felt the hope, and even the fight that we all go through to better our individual selves. I experienced everything in the book as though I was reliving some of my own struggles and challenges through those exact emotions that Naoko, Watanabe, and Midori feel through their lives. This is a story about such a far away place, and yet you'll feel so close to this book.<br /><br />The book begins with all the things he (Watanabe) has lost in the course of his life. \\"Times gone forever, friends who have died or disappeared, feelings I would never know again,\\" he says. The book concludes with possible opportunity, a new beginning, or an especially long over-due addition to his life. Quite the rollercoaster, all the while enjoying the little things that keeps us grounded, smiling and laughing, and looking forward to the tomorrow.<br /><br />Murakami has the ability to totally capture his characters. The whole time I read about the characters, one by one, I felt as though Murakami had learned about them thoroughly, knowing all their intricacies and subtleties, then placed himself in their shoes, and from that perspective told their stories as they would themselves. They are so well captured and developed. They are real people with real stories with real human spirits. The characters are diverse, unique, and genuine. They also carry a level of universalism that I have not yet read in characters up to now. To think that this all takes place in Japan boggles my mind. It felt I was reading about people I have knew or know. Naoko, Toru, Midori, and Reiko truly moved me, all in their special and unique mannerism.<br /><br />There is so much to write about this book. It's ultimately one of my most prized books, now. It's just one of those novels that I will come back to frequently. It leaves a mark on you. It is definitely bittersweet that I have finished this novel. I believe I never really wanted it to end. I wish I could know how the story continues.<br /><br />There are a lot of references to really great books in this novel. Something to keep in mind while reading this book. It will make you crave to read amazing books. Too many to sift through and list them. I do remember, however, Fitzgerald, Dostoyevsky, and Einstein being mentioned. But there are several more others.<br /><br />Murakami really gives us a perspective on the balance of life and death. The universe likes to balance things out. We all pay our dues. All these themes are part of a larger context in this book. Perhaps, this is one of the most mysterious elements to the book, never knowing how life and death are really linked to our purpose on earth. We speculate. We have theories. Some of us even have our convictions. But what is it that drives young individuals to their own demise, to suicide? Why? This book may help you see some of the details that transpire in the lives of the four people who decide to end their own lives, but it's still a mystery what really leads them there, exactly. We can speculate, and entertain several ideas, but it leaves a lot for questioning. I absolutely admire Murakami for tackling such concept with such interwoven details, and succeeding so effortlessly.<br /><br />There are just so many things to talk about with this book.... I could go on forever. I know I will be coming back to this book, sooner than I think. It's one of those books that you will crave. I have an intuition about things, much like Midori does in the book.

B00005MKA1|4.0|Bob Marley & The Wailers - Kaya (1978)<br /><br />The songs that appear on KAYA were recorded at the same time as the tracks that comprise the legendary EXODUS album, but you wouldn't necessarily know this was a collection of leftovers just from listening to it. It's a very good album. Some people claim that the rootsier early Bob Marley is the best but I actually prefer the expertly produced pop sheen of later albums like this one and especially UPRISING (my favorite Bob Marley album). Some of these songs have been recorded previously but I think the versions on KAYA are great improvements both in presentation and production. Overall the album is laid back and groovy yet there's a fair amount of diversity too. As for the sound of this latest remaster, to my ears it's excellent. I can pick out all kinds of details - it sounds crisp. Marley is without a doubt the ultimate reggae musician and one of the greatest musicians ever, period.<br /><br />Breakdown!<br /><br />\\"Easy Skanking\\" - is an enjoyable, easy going groove. I wouldn't call it \\"lazy\\" though as it's so skillfully played with a rather tricky, unconventional reggae rhythm. \\"Excuse me while I light my spliff/ Oh God I've got to take a lift/ From reality I just can't drift/ That's why I'm staying with this riff...\\" :) ****<br /><br />\\"Kaya\\" -  mellow yet upbeat,  this is a wonderful tribute to pot, a top-notch \\"feel good\\" Bob Marley tune. *****<br /><br />\\"Is This Love\\" - supremely melodic, laid-back tune that rightly earned a place on the legendary LEGEND compilation. Like many of these songs, it's actually quite sophisticated, yet Marley and band make it sound so simple and easy. *****+<br /><br />\\"Sun Is Shining\\" - this song had been around for quite a while before Bob and co. recorded the definitive version that appears here. It's incredibly hypnotic and atmospheric. Junior Marvin contributes some fantastic guitar noodling over the proceedings and the airy background vocals of the I-Threes (Rita Marley, Judy Mowatt and Marcia Griffiths) are gorgeous. *****+<br /><br />\\"Satisfy My Soul\\" - has some nice horns and an agreeable groove, nice and easy. ***3/4<br /><br />\\"She's Gone\\" - It's not one of the strongest tracks here but it's still very good. It's got a sweet reggae groove driven by guitar and Bob's melodic vocal is wonderful. ****<br /><br />\\"Misty Morning\\" - is incredible, possibly the best track on the album. It's got some great riffs played on brass instruments, a staggered, very authoritative groove. Marley really pours his guts out on this one. Definitive reggae, yet something unique. *****+<br /><br />\\"Crisis\\" - another lesser track but worthy nonetheless - there are no weak tracks on KAYA! The music is less driven yet Marley delivers a heart-felt, creative vocal. ***1/2<br /><br />\\"Running Away\\" - is probably my least favorite track but it's quality and it's in fact a highlight according to other music reviewers. 0062060562|4.0|If you have been looking for a suspenseful thriller with a new twist - then this book is for you.<br />Christine wakes up in the morning in a strange bed with no memory of how she got there or where she is. When she looks in the mirror, things turn from bad to worse - who is this middle aged stranger looking back at her? In her last memory she is 20-something and at a party with her best friend.<br />Christine soon learns some disturbing facts: the stranger in the bed next to her is in fact her husband, Ben, who she doesn't remember at all, nor feels any emotional connection to. He tells her that she has suffered a traumatic brain injury when she was 29 years old, and has been suffering from an unusual kind of amnesia ever since. Every time Christine falls asleep, her memory is wiped clean of anything that has happened to her since her early twenties, and every morning she has to rely on Ben to tell her who she is, where she is, and what has happened to her.<br />Whilst Ben is at work, Christine receives a phone call from a doctor who claims to have been in contact with her for some time to help her regain her memory. He tells her that she has been keeping a journal, writing down everything that is happening to her during the day - to replace the memories she cannot retain. When Christine looks through the journal, she reads: \\"Don't trust Ben.\\" It soon becomes apparent to her that Ben is not always telling her the truth. About her accident. About their son (Their son? How can she be a mother and not even remember her own child?). About the mysterious fire which apparently destroyed all their photos. But why would Ben lie to her?<br />Trying to find out the truth, Christine must not only do battle with her own mind playing tricks on her, but with the many inconsistencies in the \\"truths\\" told to her by other people. Especially her husband ....<br />If I could give readers any advice it would be to only pick up this book when they have no other commitments, as it is very addictive. I read it in one night long read-a-thon, so engrossed in the distorted fabric of Christine's recollections that I could not go to sleep without finding out how it would all end. And it was worth it.<br />This beautifully crafted novel challenges every belief I ever held about reality and truth and identity, and the importance of our minds to define who we are. Getting into Christine's mind is a scary journey - like a fragmented mirror only reflecting parts of a whole, her memories appear in short bursts, and she can never be sure what is real and what is not. If there was a living hell, this would surely be one of them.<br />Sure, there were some holes in the story, but all in all the plot was so original and engrossing that the book made for a great read. Christine's voice is engaging and reflects her inner confusion and torment, convincing the reader that all is not as it seems. Every time I thought I had it worked out, some new twist would appear. Though I did cotton on to the plot before all was revealed, I still felt the shock of the massive betrayal through Christine's eyes.<br />A great novel in many ways, and I thoroughly enjoyed the journey.ry addictive. B007WUV302|4.0|Storm Corrosion, the collaboration between Mikael Akerfeldt of Opeth and Steven Wilson of Porcupine Tree, offers some intriguing, unpredictable journeys into what I would describe as mood music. A few words that occur to me while listening are dreamlike, lush, serene, hypnotic, mellow, haunting, ethereal, exotic, and, at times, dark. Beautiful acoustic work and captivating ambient passages intertwine for some entrancing zone-out music; it's easy to get lost in the soft, poignant melodies of each song. For me, it took a few listens to really absorb the multiple layers of sound and appreciate how it all flows together. I listened to the first track Drag Ropes once and thought it was ok, then I listened to it a second time and thought it was pretty great. By the third time, I loved it, and the rest of the album had much the same effect. Now I have to hear it every day, sometimes more than once.<br /><br />The albums's six songs alternate between strange, surreal ambience and delicate, melancholic folk. There's a stark contrast from dark to light between the first two songs, Drag Ropes and the album's title track. Drag Ropes is at times nightmarish, especially coupled with the Tim Burton-esque video released for it, while Storm Corrosion is a beautiful track, its haunting acoustic reminiscent of Opeth's Orchid (or even Led Zeppelin or early Black Sabbath). The tone shifts back into darkness with Hag's rhythmic pulse and odd section of semi-heavy guitars and the album's only spurt of drumming. Then, Happy mixes the strangely warped acoustic sound of Watershed with lighter, Still Life melodies akin to songs like The Moor or Benighted. After this, it's back into more trippy territory with Lock Howl, a brilliant instrumental that I wish was at least twice as long as it is. Ljudet Innan closes soft and serene, with some of Mikael's most ambitious vocals to date in the opening minutes. My only complaint is that this album isn't longer, the six songs clocking in at close to 48 minutes. I would've gladly taken a couple more ten-minute tracks; I just can't seem to get enough of this music. Great stuff by Mikael and Steven.<br /><br />As for the mp3 Special Edition...where are the instrumental tracks?  Not having a Blu-ray player, I didn't buy the Blu-ray edition, which features demo versions of Drag Ropes and Hag, as well as instrumentals of all six songs on the album (Lock Howl is the same).  I've listened to all of these tracks online, and they're great, but I've been waiting for mp3's to be made available.  And now they only offer the two demo tracks for a Special Edition?  I'll definitely get these two demos, which have enough differences from the final versions to justify having them, but I'm really disappointed that I can only listen to the instrumentals online.  It would've been good for Roadrunner to release them on mp3 for those of us without Blu-ray players.  People would still be enticed to buy the Blu-ray since it has a 5.1 mix of the entire album, just like people are enticed to buy the CD since the tracks Storm Corrosion and Ljudet Innan aren't available on mp3.  So, five stars for the album itself and three for the missing instrumentals averages to four for the entire product.  They should've made all the bonus tracks available here.minutes. B000002KE2|5.0|Black Sabbath:  Vol. 4 is yet another legendary album from the original line-up of Ozzy, Tony, Geezer, and Bill.  After the dark, headbanging metal of their third album, Master of Reality, Vol. 4 sees Sabbath return to a more laid-back, groovy vibe, sounding in style, guitar tone, and vocals more like their debut album than either of its two predecessors.  It has more of a rock vibe than Paranoid or Master of Reality, with stronger blues influences.  It's definitely nowhere near as dark in tone as Master of Reality.  Although Vol. 4 contains many throwback elements to their debut, it also introduces some new ideas.  Wheels of Confusion has a tambourine-fueled hippie jam at the end, and Changes was the most ambitious balladry Sabbath had attempted up to that point.  Besides these great songs, Vol. 4 includes several classics, like Tomorrow's Dream, album highlight Supernaut, longtime live staple Snowblind, Cornucopia, the instrumental Laguna Sunrise, and frequently covered album closer Under the Sun.  Without question, Vol. 4 is a must-have for any fan of Black Sabbath or Ozzy.  The fourth release from the most important band in heavy metal history is a keeper.
